Question Text:
Here's a fun riddle that I think you'll enjoy.

You have been selected to play the final round of the hit new game show "Pick That Ping-Pong". In this round, you will be competing for a large cash prize. Your job will be to pick one of several different numbered ping-pong balls, and then the game will commence. The host describes how the game works.

A device consisting of a winding clear ramp and a series of pistons controls the outcome of the game. The ramp feeds balls onto a platform. The platform has room for three ping-pong balls at a time. The three balls on the platform are each aligned with one of three pistons. At each stage of the game, one of the three pistons will randomly fire, ejecting the ball it strikes. If the piston ejects the ball in the first position on the platform the balls in the second and third position on the platform each advance one space, and the next ball on the ramp advances to the third position. If the piston ejects the ball in the second position, the ball in the first position is released and rolls away, the ball in the third position advances two spaces to occupy the first position, and the next two balls on the ramp advance to occupy the second and third positions on the platform. If the piston ejects the ball in the third position, the ball in the first position is released and rolls away, the ball in the second position advances one space to occupy the first position, and the next two balls on the ramp advance to occupy the second and third positions on the platform.

The ramp begins with 100 numbered ping-pong balls, arranged in ascending order from 1 to 100. The host activates the machine and the first three balls, numbered 1, 2, and 3, advance to the platform. Before the random firing of the pistons begins, you are asked which of the 100 balls you would like to pick. If your pick is ejected by one of the pistons, you win the grand prize, $10,000.

Which ball should you choose to maximize your odds of winning the big prize? Please provide your answer as the number of the ball selected.

=====================================================================================================================

Authors:[Yu Ge](https://arxiv.org/search/cs?searchtype=author&query=Ge,+Y) (1), [Linna Xie](https://arxiv.org/search/cs?searchtype=author&query=Xie,+L) (1), [Zhong Li](https://arxiv.org/search/cs?searchtype=author&query=Li,+Z) (1), [Yu Pei](https://arxiv.org/search/cs?searchtype=author&query=Pei,+Y) (2), [Tian Zhang](https://arxiv.org/search/cs?searchtype=author&query=Zhang,+T) (1) ((1) Nanjing University, (2) The Hong Kong Polytechnic University)

[HTML (experimental)](https://arxiv.org/html/2509.13782v1)
> Abstract:Large Language Model Powered Multi-Agent Systems (MASs) are increasingly employed to automate complex real-world problems, such as programming and scientific discovery. Despite their promising, MASs are not without their flaws. However, failure attribution in MASs - pinpointing the specific agent actions responsible for failures - remains underexplored and labor-intensive, posing significant challenges for debugging and system improvement. To bridge this gap, we propose FAMAS, the first spectrum-based failure attribution approach for MASs, which operates through systematic traje

import re
# Find lines or entries that contain a year in parentheses and capture the surrounding text
entries = re.findall(r'([^\n]*\(\d{4}\)[^\n]*)', page)
print("Found entries count:", len(entries))

# Parse years and keep the earliest
parsed = []
for e in entries:
    m = re.search(r'\((\d{4})\)', e)
    if m:
        year = int(m.group(1))
        # Try to extract a title-like portion: text after the year or between authors and year
        # Common pattern: "Author (YEAR)  [Title - PDF]" or "Author (YEAR) Title - PDF"
        # We'll extract the substring after the year and clean brackets/links.
        after = re.split(r'\(\d{4}\)', e, maxsplit=1)[1].strip()
        # Remove surrounding punctuation
        title = re.sub(r'^\s*[-–:]*\s*', '', after)
        parsed.append((year, title, e))

# Sort by year ascending
parsed.sort(key=lambda x: x[0])
print("Earliest entries (up to 5):", parsed[:5])

if parsed:
    earliest = parsed[0]
    # The title field might contain author names again; try to further clean to get the title.
    candidate = earliest[1]
    # If there is a bracketed link like [Title](...), remove markdown link formatting leftover
    candidate = re.sub(r'\[|\]', '', candidate)
    # Remove trailing commas and - PDF and similar
    candidate = re.sub(r'\s*[-–—]\s*PDF.*$', '', candidate, flags=re.IGNORECASE)
    candidate = candidate.strip()
    print("Earliest year:", earliest[0])
    print("Candidate title snippet:", candidate)
    final_answer(candidate)
else:
    final_answer("Unable to determine")

STEP 13
============================================================
Duration: 12.15s
Tokens: input=14472, output=1308

--- Model Output ---
Thought: I'll download the BBC Writersroom PDF for the Heaven Sent script again, extract and decompress the Flate streams from the PDF, concatenate the decompressed text, and search for the first scene heading (lines beginning with INT. or EXT.). I'll print the relevant portion so I can return the exact setting as it appears in the first scene heading.

<code>
import re, zlib

# fetch PDF
pdf_raw = visit_webpage(url="http://downloads.bbc.co.uk/writersroom/scripts/DW9-EP11-Heaven-Sent.pdf")

# convert to bytes preserving raw values
pdf_bytes = pdf_raw.encode('latin-1')

# find all stream...endstream blocks
streams = re.findall(b'stream\\r?\\n(.*?)endstream', pdf_bytes, re.S)

decompressed = []
for i, s in enumerate(streams):
    try:
        # Some streams may have extra newlines; try to strip trailing whitespace
        comp = s.strip(b'\r\n')

        first_scene_heading = m.group(0).strip()
        break

        first_scene_heading = m.group(0).strip()
        break

Doctor Who s09e11 Episode Script
================================

### Heaven Sent

As you come into this world, something else is also born.  
 You begin your life, and it begins a journey towards you.  
 It moves slowly, but it never stops.  
 Wherever you go, whatever path you take, it will follow.  
 Never faster, never slower, always coming.  
 You will run.  
 It will walk.  
 You will rest.  
 It will not.  
 One day, you will linger in the same place too long.  
 You will sit too still or sleep too deep .  
.  
 and when, too late, you rise to go .  
.  
 you will notice a second shadow next to yours.  
 Your life will then be over.  
 If you think because she is dead, I am weak, then you understand very little.  
 If you were any part of killing her, and you're not afraid, then you understand nothing at all.  
 So, for your own sake, understand this.  
 I am the Doctor.  
 I'm coming to find you, and I will never, ever stop.

import re

pdf_raw = visit_webpage(url="http://downloads.bbc.co.uk/writersroom/scripts/DW9-EP11-Heaven-Sent.pdf")

# search for INT. and EXT. occurrences
occurrences = []
for m in re.finditer(r'(?:\n|\r\n|\r|^).{0,40}(INT\.|EXT\.).{0,120}', pdf_text):
    occurrences.append((m.start(), m.group(0)))

# also search for likely location words
words = ["CASTLE", "MAZE", "WALL", "DIAL", "CONFESSION", "CAVERN", "CLOISTER", "HALL"]
word_hits = []
for w in words:
    for m in re.finditer(w, pdf_text, re.IGNORECASE):

        end = min(len(pdf_text), m.end()+60)
        word_hits.append(pdf_text[start:end])

print("Found INT./EXT. context snippets (up to 10):")
for i, oc in enumerate(occurrences[:10]):

    print(oc[1])
    print()

print("Found keyword hits (up to 10):")
for i, snippet in enumerate(word_hits[:10]):
    print(f"--- hit {i+1} ---")
    print(snippet)
    print()
</code>

(A → B) ↔ (¬A ∨ B)
(¬A → B) ↔ (A ∨ ¬B)
¬(A → B) ↔ (A ∧ ¬B)

Which of the above is not logically equivalent to the rest? Provide the full statement that doesn't fit.

Detected requirements: {'unit_scale': None, 'format_type': 'text', 'separator': ', ', 'order': None, 'precision': None, 'specificity': None, 'numeric_only': False}

--- Model Output ---
Thought: I'll test each biconditional by evaluating its truth value for all possible truth assignments of A and B. The one that is not a tautology (false for some assignment) is the statement that doesn't fit. I'll use Python to check the four possible valuations.

<code>
import itertools

def impl(p, q):
    return (not p) or q

statements = [
    ("¬(A ∧ B) ↔ (¬A ∨ ¬B)",
     lambda A,B: not (A and B),
     lambda A,B: (not A) or (not B)),
    ("¬(A ∨ B) ↔ (¬A ∧ ¬B)",
     lambda A,B: not (A or B),
     lambda A,B: (not A) and (not B)),
    ("(A → B) ↔ (¬B → ¬A)",
     lambda A,B: impl(A,B),
     lambda A,B: impl(not B, not A)),
    ("(A → B) ↔ (¬A ∨ B)",
     lambda A,B: impl(A,B),
     lambda A,B: (not A) or B),
    ("(¬A → B) ↔ (A ∨ ¬B)",
     lambda A,B: impl(not A, B),
     lambda A,B: A or (not B)),
    ("¬(A → B) ↔ (A ∧ ¬B)",
     lambda A,B: not impl(A,B),
     lambda A,B: A and (not B)),
]

results = {}
for name, left, right in statements:
    is_taut = True
    counterexamples = []
    for A, B in itertools.product([False, True], repeat=2):
        L = left(A,B)
        R = right(A,B)
        if L != R:
            is_taut = False
            counterexamples.append((A,B,L,R))
    results[name] = (is_taut, counterexamples)
    print(f"Statement: {name}")
    print("  Tautology?" , is_taut)
    if not is_taut:
        print("  Counterexamples (A, B, Left, Right):", counterexamples)
    print()

# Identify the one that is not a tautology
not_taut = [name for name,(t,_) in results.items() if not t]
print("Not tautological statements:", not_taut)

# Final answer
final_answer(not_taut[0])
</code>

My family reunion is this week, and I was assigned the mashed potatoes to bring. The attendees include my married mother and father, my twin brother and his family, my aunt and her family, my grandma and her brother, her brother's daughter, and his daughter's family. All the adults but me have been married, and no one is divorced or remarried, but my grandpa and my grandma's sister-in-law passed away last year. All living spouses are attending. My brother has two children that are still kids, my aunt has one six-year-old, and my grandma's brother's daughter has three kids under 12. I figure each adult will eat about 1.5 potatoes of mashed potatoes and each kid will eat about 1/2 a potato of mashed potatoes, except my second cousins don't eat carbs. The average potato is about half a pound, and potatoes are sold in 5-pound bags. How many whole bags of potatoes do I need? Just give the number.

Thought: I'll compute the number of adults and kids who will eat, calculate total potatoes needed, convert to pounds, then divide by 5-pound bags and round up to whole bags. I'll do the arithmetic in Python and return just the final number of bags.

<code>
import math

# Counts derived from the problem description
me = 1
parents = 2  # mother and father
brother = 1
brother_spouse = 1
brother_kids = 2  # kids that will eat
aunt = 1
aunt_spouse = 1
aunt_kid = 1  # six-year-old
grandma = 1
grandma_brother = 1
niece = 1  # grandma's brother's daughter
niece_spouse = 1
niece_kids = 3  # these are second cousins and don't eat carbs

# Adults attending (all living spouses attend; some spouses deceased as described)
adults = (me + parents + brother + brother_spouse + aunt + aunt_spouse +
          grandma + grandma_brother + niece + niece_spouse)

# Kids who will eat (exclude second cousins who don't eat carbs)
kids_eating = brother_kids + aunt_kid  # niece_kids don't eat carbs

# Consumption rates
potatoes_per_adult = 1.5
potatoes_per_kid = 0.5

total_potatoes = adults * potatoes_per_adult + kids_eating * potatoes_per_kid
print("Total potatoes (count):", total_potatoes)

# Weight calculations
avg_potato_weight_lb = 0.5
total_pounds = total_potatoes * avg_potato_weight_lb
print("Total pounds:", total_pounds)

# Bags needed (5-pound bags), rounded up
bags_needed = math.ceil(total_pounds / 5)
print("Bags needed:", bags_needed)

final_answer("2")
</code>

import re
# use the previously retrieved page variable
try:
    text = page  # page was set in an earlier step
except NameError:
    text = visit_webpage(url="https://journal.finfar.org/articles/dragons-are-tricksy-the-uncanny-dragons-of-childrens-literature/")

# extract quoted substrings (both "..." and ‘...’ and '...')
quotes = re.findall(r'["\u201c\u201d\']([^"\u201c\u201d\']+?)["\u201c\u201d\']', text)
# filter to single-word quotes (no spaces) and normalize
single_word_quotes = [q.strip() for q in quotes if len(q.split())==1]
from collections import Counter
counts = Counter(single_word_quotes)
# show most common
print("Most common single-word quoted substrings (top 30):")
for w, c in counts.most_common(30):
    print(f"{w!r}: {c}")

<code>
import re

# ensure we have the page text
try:
    text = page
except NameError:
    text = visit_webpage(url="https://journal.finfar.org/articles/dragons-are-tricksy-the-uncanny-dragons-of-childrens-literature/")

def contexts_for(term, radius=150):
    outs = []
    for m in re.finditer(re.escape(term), text, flags=re.IGNORECASE):
        start = max(0, m.start()-radius)
        end = min(len(text), m.end()+radius)
        outs.append(text[start:end].replace("\n", " "))
    return outs

terms = ["bemoan", "bemoaned", "soften", "softened", "satirize", "satirised", "tame", "tamed", "cute", 
         "sentimental", "saccharine", "fluffy", "ruin", "ruined", "unnatural", "domestic", "domesticated",
         "banal", "cuddly", "tricksy"]

for t in terms:
    ctxs = contexts_for(t)
    if ctxs:
        print(f"\n--- Contexts for '{t}' ({len(ctxs)} hits) ---")
        for c in ctxs[:5]:
            print("...", c, "...")
</code>

def contexts_for(term, radius=150):
    outs = []
    for m in re.finditer(re.escape(term), text, flags=re.IGNORECASE):
        start = max(0, m.start()-radius)
        end = min(len(text), m.end()+radius)
        outs.append(text[start:end].replace("\n", " "))

Question Text:
In the fictional language of Tizin, basic sentences are arranged with the Verb first, followed by the direct object, followed by the subject of the sentence. I want to express my love for apples to my Tizin friend. 

The word that indicates oneself is "Pa" is the nominative form, "Mato" is the accusative form, and "Sing" is the genitive form. 

The root verb that indicates an intense like for something is "Maktay". When it is used in the present, it is used in it's root form, when it is used in the preterit past, it is "Tay", and when it is used in the imperfect past, it is "Aktay". It is used differently than in English, and is better translated as "is pleasing to", meaning that the thing doing the liking is actually the object of the sentence rather than the subject.

The word for apples is borrowed from English in Tizin, and so it is "Apple" is the nominative form, "Zapple" is the accusative form, and "Izapple" is the genitive form. 

Please translate "I like apples" to Tizin.

Detected requirements: {'unit_scale': None, 'format_type': 'text', 'separator': ', ', 'order': None, 'precision': None, 'specificity': None, 'numeric_only': False}

Because "Maktay" means "is pleasing to," the person doing the liking ("I") is treated as the object (accusative: "Mato"), and apples are the subject (nominative: "Apple"). So the Tizin sentence is: Maktay Mato Apple.

def find_all(s, sub):
    starts = []
    start = 0
    while True:
        idx = s.find(sub, start)
        if idx == -1:
            break
        starts.append(idx)
        start = idx + len(sub)
    return starts

markers = ["—", "<cite", "By ", "by ", "— ", "Source:", "quoted", "—\n"]
for m in markers:
    idxs = find_all(page, m)
    if not idxs:
        continue

        end = min(len(page), idx+200)
        print(f"\nContext {i+1} (pos {idx}):\n")
        print(page[start:end])
        print("\n" + "-"*60)
</code>

Other predictors

* Custom metrics for the `neighbors` binary trees now have
  fewer constraints: they must take two 1d-arrays and return a float.
  [#6288](https://github.com/scikit-learn/scikit-learn/issues/6288) by [Jake Vanderplas](https://staff.washington.edu/jakevdp/).
* `algorithm='auto` in `neighbors` estimators now chooses the most
  appropriate algorithm for all input types and metrics. [#9145](https://github.com/scikit-learn/scikit-learn/issues/9145) by
  [Herilalaina Rakotoarison](https://github.com/herilalaina) and [Reddy Chinthala](https://github.com/preddy5).

The only rule restricting the host's coin placement is that one box must contain at least 2 coins, and one box must contain 6 more coins than another box. import re

# fre_page was fetched previously; if not present, fetch it again.
try:
    fre_page  # use existing variable
except NameError:
    fre_page = visit_webpage(url="https://www.law.cornell.edu/rules/fre")

# Split the page by article headings to group rules by article
article_chunks = re.split(r'(\d+\.\s+ARTICLE\s+[IVXLC]+\. [^\n]+)', fre_page)
# The split keeps separators; reconstruct (heading, content) pairs
articles = []
for i in range(1, len(article_chunks), 2):
    heading = article_chunks[i].strip()
    content = article_chunks[i+1]
    # Extract article roman numeral for clarity
    m = re.search(r'ARTICLE\s+([IVXLC]+)\.\s+([^\n]+)', heading)
    art_num = m.group(1) if m else heading
    art_name = m.group(2) if m else heading
    # Find all rules in this chunk: pattern like [Rule 601](/rules/fre/rule_601). Title
    rules = re.findall(r'\[Rule\s+(\d+)\]\(/rules/fre/rule_\1\)\.\s*([^\n<]+)', content)
    # Save as list of (rule_number, title)
    articles.append({'article_num': art_num, 'article_name': art_name.strip(), 'rules': rules})

# Count titles containing "witnesses" (case-insensitive) per article
for art in articles:
    count = 0
    matches = []
    for rn, title in art['rules']:
        if re.search(r'\bwitnesses\b', title, re.IGNORECASE):
            count += 1
            matches.append((rn, title.strip()))
    art['witnesses_count'] = count
    art['witnesses_matches'] = matches

# Print counts for inspection
for art in articles:
    print(f"Article {art['article_num']} ({art['article_name']}): {art['witnesses_count']} titles with 'witnesses'")
    for m in art['witnesses_matches']:
        print("  ", m)
    print(

List of Presidential Birthplaces — Roadtrip to their Birthplaces, Historic Sites, and Homes
===========================================================================================

* [Cards](/travel/list-of-presidential-birthplaces-roadtrip-to-their-birthplaces-historic-sites-and-homes?layout=nomap#cards)
* [List](/travel/list-of-presidential-birthplaces-roadtrip-to-their-birthplaces-historic-sites-and-homes?layout=nomap#sitelist)
* [Map](/travel/list-of-presidential-birthplaces-roadtrip-to-their-birthplaces-historic-sites-and-homes)

#### Let us look and take a tour across the country to see the birthplaces of the 46 United States Presidents. Look back into the yesteryears and visit the presidential birthplaces and early childhood historic sites.

#### Find out where the Presidents of the United States were born and their past before becoming a great part of our history.

#### If you have any suggestions or additional information related to this post, please add them in the comments below or [send us a](mailto:Editors@TheHistoryList.com) [message](http://Editors@TheHistoryList.com). If we publish a trip as a result, we'll include your name in the credits.

#### **GEORGE WASHINGTON** (February 22, 1732)

#### [George Washington Birthplace National Monument](https://www.thehistorylist.com/sites/george-washington-birthplace-national-monument-colonial-beach-virginia/list-of-presidential-birthplaces-roadtrip-to-their-birthplaces-historic-sites-and-homes-c8e604fc-ba01-4d1f-8a27-215bd7094917)

One of the Founding Fathers of the new United States of America and the nation’s first president, George Washington was born on February 22, 1732, at Pope’s Creek Estate, near to what is now Colonial Beach in Westmoreland County, Virginia. The site is now the [George Washington Birthplace National Monument](https://www.thehistorylist.com/sites/george-washington-birthplace-national-monument-colonial-beach-virginia/list-of-presidential-birthplaces-roadtrip-to-their-birthplaces-historic-sites-and-homes-c8e604fc-ba01-4d1f-8a27-215bd7094917), which the public can visit and learn more about our first president’s past. It is a beautiful 550 acres of the former Pope’s Creek Plantation, the ancestral dwelling of the Washington family. The place also offers various interesting sites and activities for the whole family, such as historic structures, picnic grounds, a visitor center, a colonial-inspired fa

=== PAGE: Quincy, Massachusetts ===

Quincy (English pronunciation: /ˈkwɪnzi/ ) is a city in Norfolk County, Massachusetts, United States. It is the largest city in the county. Quincy is part of the Greater Boston area as one of Boston's immediate southern suburbs. Its population in 2020 was 101,636, making it the seventh-largest city in the state. Known as the "City of Presidents", Quincy is the birthplace of two U.S. presidents—John Adams and his son John Quincy Adams—as well as John Hancock, the first signer of the Declaration of Independence and the first and third governor of Massachusetts.
First settled in 1625, Quincy was briefly part of Dorchester before becoming the North Precinct of Braintree in 1640. In 1792, Quincy was split off from the Town of Braintree and was incorporated separately as the Town of Quincy; the

=== PAGE: Braintree, Massachusetts ===

Braintree (US:  ) is a town in Norfolk County, Massachusetts, United States. It is officially known as a town, but Braintree is a city with a mayor–council form of government, and it is considered a city under Massachusetts law. The population was 39,143 at the 2020 census. It is part of the Greater Boston area, with access to the MBTA Red Line, and is a member of the Metropolitan Area Planning Council's South Shore Coalition. The first mayor of Braintree was Joe Sullivan, who served until January 2020. The current mayor of Braintree is Erin Joyce, who was elected in 2023, defeating incumbent Charles Kokoros.

Father Son and Holy Ghost
=========================

F

[![Audre Lorde](/storage/poets/resize/150x150_Mucm2fiEV5KhSB2HtZMj4ei3pQWw2UuK3eIpiYPr.jpeg) By

Audre Lorde](/poets/wgti-audre-lorde/ "Audre Lorde")

I have not ever seen my father’s grave.

  

Not that his judgment eyes

have been forgotten

nor his great hands’ print

on our evening doorknobs

one half turn each night

and he would come

drabbled with the world’s business

massive and silent

as the whole day’s wish

ready to redefine

each of our shapes

but now the evening doorknobs

wait and do not recognize us

as we pass.

  

Each week a different woman

regular as his one quick glass

each evening

pulls up the grass his stillness grows

calling it weed.

Each week a different woman

has my mother’s face

and he

who [time](/category/time/ "time") has changeless

must be amazed

who knew and loved

but one.

  

My [father](/category/father/ "father") died in silence

loving creation

and well-defined response

he lived still judgments

on familiar things

and died knowing

a [january](/category/january/ "january") 15th that year me.

  

Lest I go into dust

I have not ever seen my father’s grave.

3.1K

Father Son and Holy Ghost
=========================

###### By Audre Lorde

I have not ever seen my father’s grave.  
  
Not that his judgment eyes  
have been forgotten  
nor his great hands’ print  
on our evening doorknobs  
            one half turn each night  
            and he would come  
            drabbled with the world’s business     
            massive and silent  
            as the whole day’s wish     
            ready to redefine  
            each of our shapes  
but now the evening doorknobs     
wait    and do not recognize us     
as we pass.  
  
Each week a different woman     
regular as his one quick glass  
each evening  
pulls up the grass his stillness grows     
calling it weed.  
Each week    a different woman     
has my mother’s face  
and he  
who time has    changeless  
must be amazed  
who knew and loved  
but one.  
  
My father died in silence     
loving creation  
and well-defined response     
he lived    still judgments     
on familiar things  
and died    knowing  
a January 15th that year me.  
  
Lest I go into dust  
I have not ever seen my father’s grave.

Audre Lorde, “Father Son and Holy Ghost” from *Collected Poems of Audre Lorde.* Copyright © 1997 by The Audre Lorde Estate. Reprinted with the permission of W. W. Norton & Company, Inc. This selection may not be reproduced, stored in a retrieval system, or transmitted in any form or by any means without the prior written permission of the publisher.

### Poet Bio

![Image of Audre Lorde.](https://cdn-test.poetryfoundation.org/cdn-cgi/image/g=0.2606x0.2696,w=10,h=10,q=50,fit=cover,blur=3/qa/content/images/2f93a5cfe6d0cf4d4e1a4eec2da605c22ca54c0a.jpeg)

The impassioned poetry of Audre Lorde grew out of her keen sense of injustice—racial as well as gender—and a strong desire to break through silence and politeness to unafraid illumination. Born in New York City to West Indian parents, she turned in her later work to African sources, emphasizing its oral roots and finding a model in the matriarchies of that continent for her emergent lesbian and communal consciousness.

There Are Hundreds of Mysterious Filaments at the Center of the Milky Way
=========================================================================

By [Carolyn Collins Petersen](/authors/cc-petersen.html) - June 06, 2023 06:00 PM UTC | [Milky Way](/categories/milky-way.html)

[![](/article_images/MeerKAT_and_bubbles_composite-1030x618-1.png)](/article_images/MeerKAT_and_bubbles_composite-1030x618-1.png)

Several million years ago, the core of our galaxy experienced a powerful event. It blew out a huge bi-lobed bubble that blasted through the interstellar medium in two directions. Whatever it was, it released huge amounts of energy from the central supermassive black hole, Sagittarius A\* (Sgr A\* for short).

These bubbles contain weird, spindly one-dimensional vertical filaments that emit radio signals of their own. In 1984, astronomer Farhad Yusef-Zadeh at Northwestern University and his collaborators were [the first to observe these wispy trails](https://www.universetoday.com/154593/imaging-the-galaxys-centre-in-unprecedented-detail-reveals-more-mysterious-filaments/). Now, they know of more than a thousand of them. They give off radio waves in the microwave portion of the electromagnetic spectrum.

[![MeerKAT image of the galactic center with color-coded position angles of the vertical filaments. Courtesy Farhad Yusef-Zadeh/Northwestern University](/article_images/2cd8b0ca-e457-417f-abd6-f7cecf5b03fd.png)](/article_images/2cd8b0ca-e457-417f-abd6-f7cecf5b03fd.png)

Wait, There's More!
-------------------

Recently, the team found a smaller population of filaments near Sgr A\*. However, these aren't vertical. Instead, they lie along the galactic plane and radiate away like spokes on a wheel. "It was a surprise to suddenly find a new population of structures that seem to be pointing in the direction of the black hole," Yusef-Zadeh said. "We have always been thinking about vertical filaments and their origin. I'm used to them being vertical. I never considered there might be others along the plane."

He and the team were stunned when they saw these radially stretched structures. "We had to do a lot of work to establish that we weren't fooling ourselves," he said. "And we found that these filaments are not random but appear to be tied to the outflow of our black hole. By studying them, we could learn more about the black hole's spin and accretion disk orientation. It is satisfying when one finds order in a middle of a chaotic field of the nucleus of our galaxy."

Yusef-Zadeh pointed out that something clearly occurred in the core to create the short
... [truncated]

Title:The Population of the Galactic Center Filaments: Position Angle Distribution Reveal a Degree-scale Collimated Outflow from Sgr A\* along the Galactic Plane

[Hokkaido Nippon-Ham Fighters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hokkaido_Nippon-Ham_Fighters)
The Hokkaido Nippon-Ham Fighters (北海道日本ハムファイターズ, Hokkaidō Nippon-Hamu Faitāzu) are a Japanese professional baseball team based in Kitahiroshima, Hokkaidō, in the Sapporo metropolitan area. They compete in the Pacific League of Nippon Professional Baseball, playing the majority of their home games at ES CON Field Hokkaido. The Fighters also host a select number of regional home games in cities across Hokkaidō, including Hakodate, Asahikawa, Kushiro, and Obihiro. The team's name comes from its parent organization, Nippon Ham, a major Japanese food-processing company.Founded in 1946, the Fighters called Tokyo home for 58 years, as co-tenants of the Tokyo Dome and Korakuen Stadium with the Central League's Yomiuri Giants near the end of their tenure in the capital city. The franchise has won three Japan Series titles, in 1962, 2006, and, most recently, 2016.

== Biography ==
Flor studied violin and clarinet at the Robert Schumann Conservatory in Zwickau.  He continued his music studies at the Franz Liszt Academy of Music in Weimar and the HMT Felix Mendelssohn Bartholdy in Leipzig.  He was later a conducting student with Rolf Reuter and with Kurt Masur.
